codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sleep_Deprivation_%E2%80%93_Shift_Work_%26_Long_Work_Hours_Put_Nurses_at_Risk.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A video describing occupational hazards that exist among nurses</div></div></div>
<p>Internationally, there is a serious shortage of nurses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> One reason for this shortage is due to the work environment in which nurses practice. In a recent review of the empirical human factors and ergonomic literature specific to nursing performance, nurses were found to work in generally poor environmental conditions. Some countries and states have passed legislation regarding acceptable nurse-to-patient ratios.
</p><p>The fast-paced and unpredictable nature of health care places nurses at risk for injuries and illnesses, including high <a href="/wiki/Occupational_stress" title="Occupational stress">occupational stress</a>. Nursing is a particularly stressful profession, and nurses consistently identify stress as a major work-related concern and have among the highest levels of occupational stress when compared to other professions. This stress is caused by the environment, psychosocial stressors, and the demands of nursing, including new technology that must be mastered, the emotional labor involved in nursing, physical labor, <a href="/wiki/Shift_work" title="Shift work">shift work</a>, and high workload. This stress puts nurses at risk for short-term and long-term health problems, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sleep_disorders" title="Sleep disorders">sleep disorders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">mortality</a>, psychiatric disorders, stress-related illnesses, and illness in general. Nurses are at risk of developing <a href="/wiki/Compassion_fatigue" title="Compassion fatigue">compassion fatigue</a> and moral <a href="/wiki/Distress_(medicine)" title="Distress (medicine)">distress</a>, which can worsen mental health. They also have very high rates of <a href="/wiki/Occupational_burnout" title="Occupational burnout">occupational burnout</a> (40%) and <a href="/wiki/Emotional_exhaustion" title="Emotional exhaustion">emotional exhaustion</a> (43.2%). Burnout and exhaustion increase the risk for illness, medical error, and suboptimal care provision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nurses are also at risk for violence and abuse in the workplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Violence is typically perpetrated by non-staff (e.g. patients or family), whereas abuse is typically perpetrated by other hospital personnel. Of American nurses, 57% reported in 2011 that they had been threatened at work; 17% were physically assaulted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are 3 different types of workplace violence that nurses can experience. First, physical violence, which can be hitting, kicking, beating, punching, biting, and using objects to inflict force upon someone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Second, psychological violence is when something is done to impair another person through threats and/or coercion. Third, sexual violence which can include any completed or attempted non-consensual sexual act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Workplace violence an also be categorized into two different levels, interpersonal violence and organizational coercion. Interpersonal violence could be committed by co-workers and/or patients by others in the hospital. The main form of this level is verbal abuse. Organizational coercion may include an irrationally high workload, forced shifts, forced placement in different wards of the hospital, low salaries, denial of benefits for overwork, poor working environment, and other workplace stressors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> These problems can affect the quality of life for these nurses who may experience them. It can be extremely detrimental to nurses if their managers lack understanding of the severity of these problems and do not support the nurses through them.
</p><p>There are many contributing factors to workplace violence. These factors can be divided into environmental, organizational, and individual psychosocial. The environmental factors can include the specific setting (for example the emergency department), long patient wait times, frequent interruptions, uncertainty regarding the patients' treatment, and heavy workloads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-82">[82]</a></sup> Organizational factors can include inefficient teamwork, organizational injustice, lack of aggression and/or stress management programs, and distrust between colleagues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-82">[82]</a></sup> Individual psychosocial factors may include nurses being young and inexperienced, previous experiences with violence, and a lack of communication skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-82">[82]</a></sup> Misunderstandings may also occur due to the communication barrier between nurses and patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-83">[83]</a></sup> An example of this could be the patient's condition being affected by medication, pain, and/or anxiety.
</p><p>There are many causes of workplace violence. The most common perpetrators for harassment and/or bullying against nursing students were registered nurses including preceptors, mentors, and clinical facilitators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-83">[83]</a></sup> However, the main cause of workplace violence against nurses were patients. 80% of serious violence incidents in health care centers were due to the nurses' interactions with patients.
</p><p>There are many different effects of workplace violence in the field of Nursing. Workplace violence can have a negative impact on nurses both emotionally and physically. They feel depersonalized, dehumanized, fatigued, worn out, stressed out, and tired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Because of the severity of some incidents of violence, nurses have reported manifestations of burn-out due to the frequent exposure. This can heavily impact of a nurses' mental health and cause nurses to feel unsatisfied with their profession and unsafe in their work environment.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are a number of interventions that can mitigate the occupational hazards of nursing. They can be individual-focused or organization-focused. Individual-focused interventions include <a href="/wiki/Stress_management" title="Stress management">stress management</a> programs, which can be customized to individuals. Stress management programs can reduce anxiety, sleep disorders, and other symptoms of stress. Organizational interventions focus on reducing stressful aspects of the work environment by defining stressful characteristics and developing solutions to them. Using organizational and individual interventions together is most effective at reducing stress on nurses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-77">[77]</a></sup> In some Japanese hospitals, <a href="/wiki/Powered_exoskeleton#Medical" title="Powered exoskeleton">powered exoskeletons</a> are used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Lumbar supports (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Back_belt" title="Back belt">back belts</a>) have also been trialed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Worldwide">Worldwide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Worldwide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Americas">Americas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Americas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poston,_Arizona._Registered_nurse,_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser,_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu,_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2992" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg/220px-Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg/330px-Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg/440px-Poston%2C_Arizona._Registered_nurse%2C_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser%2C_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu%2C_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poston,_Arizona._Registered_nurse,_Mrs._Edythe_Sasser,_is_assisited_by_Yoshiko_Konatsu,_nurse%27s_aid_._._._-_NARA_-_537104.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two nurses in Arizona, 1943</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nursing_in_the_United_States" title="Nursing in the United States">Nursing in the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_nursing_in_the_United_States" title="History of nursing in the United States">History of nursing in the United States</a></div>
<p>In the US, scope of practice is determined by the state or territory in which a nurse is licensed. Each state has its own laws, rules, and regulations governing nursing care. Usually the making of such rules and regulations is delegated to a state <a href="/wiki/Board_of_nursing" title="Board of nursing">board of nursing</a>, which performs day-to-day administration of these rules, licenses for nurses and nursing assistants, and makes decisions on nursing issues. In some states, the terms "nurse" or "nursing" may only be used in conjunction with the practice of a <a href="/wiki/Registered_nurse" title="Registered nurse">registered nurse</a> (RN) or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Licensed_Practical_Nurse" title="Licensed Practical Nurse">licensed practical or vocational nurse</a> (LPN/LVN).
</p><p>In the hospital setting, registered nurses often delegate tasks to LPNs and <a href="/wiki/Unlicensed_assistive_personnel" title="Unlicensed assistive personnel">unlicensed assistive personnel</a>.
</p><p>RNs are not limited to employment as bedside nurses. They are employed by physicians, attorneys, insurance companies, governmental agencies, community/public health agencies, private industry, school districts, ambulatory surgery centers, among others. Some registered nurses are independent consultants who <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-employed" title="Self-employed">work for themselves</a>, while others work for large manufacturers or chemical companies. Research nurses conduct or assist in the conduct of research or evaluation (outcome and process) in many areas such as biology, psychology, human development, and health care systems.
</p><p>Many employers offer flexible work schedules, child care, educational benefits, and bonuses. About 21 percent of registered nurses are union members or covered by union contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_States_Department_of_Labor_Bureau_of_Labor_Statistics_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_Department_of_Labor_Bureau_of_Labor_Statistics-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nursing is the nation's largest health care profession. In 2017, there were more than 4,015,250 registered nurses and 922,196 licensed practical nurses nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Of all licensed RNs, 2.6 million or 84.8% are employed in nursing. Nurses comprise the largest single component of hospital staff, are the primary providers of hospital patient care, and deliver most of the nation's long-term care. The primary pathway to professional nursing, as compared to technical-level practice, is the four-year Bachelor of Science in nursing (BSN) degree. Registered nurses are prepared either through a BSN program; a three-year associate degree in nursing; or a three-year hospital training program, receiving a hospital diploma. All take the same state licensing exam. (The number of diploma programs has declined steadily—to less than 10 percent of all basic RN education programs—as nursing education has shifted from hospital-operated instruction into the college and university system.)
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_and_licensure_requirements">Educational and licensure requirements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Educational and licensure requirements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Diploma_in_Nursing">Diploma in Nursing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Diploma in Nursing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diploma_in_Nursing" title="Diploma in Nursing">Diploma in Nursing</a></div>
<p>The oldest method of nursing education is the hospital-based diploma program, which lasts approximately three years. Students take between 30 and 60 credit hours in <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nutrition" title="Nutrition">nutrition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, and other subjects at a college or university, then move on to intensive nursing classes. Until 1996, most RNs in the US were initially educated in nursing by diploma programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANA_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANA-89">[89]</a></sup> According to the Health Services Resources Administration's 2000 Survey of Nurses only six percent of nurses who graduated from nursing programs in the United States received their education at a Diploma School of Nursing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HRSA_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-HRSA-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Associate_Degree_in_Nursing">Associate Degree in Nursing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Associate Degree in Nursing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Associate_of_Science_in_Nursing" title="Associate of Science in Nursing">Associate of Science in Nursing</a></div>
<p>The most common initial nursing education is a two-year <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Associate_Degree_in_Nursing" title="Associate Degree in Nursing">Associate Degree in Nursing</a> (Associate of Applied Science in Nursing, Associate of Science in nursing, Associate Degree in Nursing), a <a href="/wiki/Community_college" title="Community college">two-year college</a> degree referred to as an ADN. Some four-year colleges and universities also offer the ADN. Associate degree nursing programs have prerequisite and corequisite courses (which may include English, Math and Human Anatomy and Physiology) and ultimately stretch out the degree-acquiring process to about three years or greater.
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Bachelor_of_Science_in_Nursing">Bachelor of Science in Nursing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Bachelor of Science in Nursing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science_in_Nursing" title="Bachelor of Science in Nursing">Bachelor of Science in Nursing</a></div>
<p>Another pathway into the profession, or a higher level of education for other nurses, is obtaining a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Science_in_Nursing" title="Bachelor of Science in Nursing">Bachelor of Science in Nursing</a> (BSN), a four-year degree that also prepares nurses for graduate-level education. For the first two years in a BSN program, students usually obtain general education requirements and spend the remaining time in nursing courses. In some new programs the first two years can be substituted for an active LPN license along with the required general studies. Advocates for the ADN and diploma programs state that such programs have an on the job training approach to educating students, while the BSN is an <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">academic degree</a> that emphasizes research and <a href="/wiki/Nursing_theory" title="Nursing theory">nursing theory</a>. Some states require a specific amount of clinical experience that is the same for both BSN and ADN students. A BSN degree qualifies its holder for administrative, research, consulting and teaching positions that would not usually be available to those with an ADN, but is not necessary for most patient care functions. Nursing schools may be accredited by either the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accreditation_Commission_for_Education_in_Nursing" title="Accreditation Commission for Education in Nursing">Accreditation Commission for Education in Nursing</a> (ACEN) or the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_Collegiate_Nursing_Education" title="Commission on Collegiate Nursing Education">Commission on Collegiate Nursing Education</a> (CCNE).
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Graduate_education">Graduate education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Graduate education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Science_in_Nursing" title="Master of Science in Nursing">Master of Science in Nursing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Nursing_Practice" title="Doctor of Nursing Practice">Doctor of Nursing Practice</a></div>
<p>Advanced education in nursing is done at the master's and doctoral levels. It prepares the graduate for specialization as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_practice_registered_nurse" title="Advanced practice registered nurse">advanced practice registered nurse</a> (APRN) or for advanced roles in leadership, management, or education. The <a href="/wiki/Clinical_nurse_leader" title="Clinical nurse leader">clinical nurse leader</a> (CNL) is an advanced generalist who focuses on the improvement of quality and safety outcomes for patients or patient populations from an administrative and staff management focus. Doctoral programs in nursing prepare the student for work in nursing education, health care administration, clinical research, public policy, or advanced clinical practice. Most programs confer the PhD in nursing or <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Nursing_Practice" title="Doctor of Nursing Practice">Doctor of Nursing Practice</a> (DNP).
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Advanced_practice_registered_nurse" title="Advanced practice registered nurse">Advanced practice registered nurse</a> (APRN)
</p><p>Areas of advanced nursing practice include that of a <a href="/wiki/Nurse_practitioner" title="Nurse practitioner">nurse practitioner</a> (NP), a certified <a href="/wiki/Nurse_midwife" title="Nurse midwife">nurse midwife</a> (CNM), a <a href="/wiki/Nurse_anesthetist" title="Nurse anesthetist">certified registered nurse anesthetist</a> (CRNA), or a <a href="/wiki/Clinical_nurse_specialist" title="Clinical nurse specialist">clinical nurse specialist</a> (CNS). Nurse practitioners and CNSs work assessing, diagnosing and treating patients in fields as diverse as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Family_practice" title="Family practice">family practice</a>, women's health care, <a href="/wiki/Emergency_nursing" title="Emergency nursing">emergency nursing</a>, acute/critical care, <a href="/wiki/Psychiatry" title="Psychiatry">psychiatry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geriatrics" title="Geriatrics">geriatrics</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a>, additionally, a CNS usually works for a facility to improve patient care, do research, or as a staff educator.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Licensure_examination">Licensure examination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Licensure examination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Completion of any one of these three educational routes allows a graduate nurse to take the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NCLEX-RN" title="NCLEX-RN">NCLEX-RN</a>, the test for <a href="/wiki/Nurse_licensure" title="Nurse licensure">licensure</a> as a registered nurse, and is accepted by every state as an adequate indicator of minimum competency for a new graduate. However, controversy exists over the appropriate entry-level preparation of RNs. Some professional organizations believe the BSN should be the sole method of RN preparation and ADN graduates should be licensed as "technical nurses" to work under the supervision of BSN graduates. Others feel the on-the-job experiences of diploma and ADN graduates makes up for any deficiency in theoretical preparation.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Shortage_in_the_United_States">Shortage in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Shortage in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nursing_shortage" title="Nursing shortage">Nursing shortage</a></div>
<p>RNs are the largest group of health care workers in the United States, with about 2.7 million employed in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bls.gov_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-bls.gov-91">[91]</a></sup> It has been reported<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2012)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> that the number of new graduates and foreign-trained nurses is insufficient to meet the <a href="/wiki/Demand" title="Demand">demand</a> for registered nurses; this is often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Nursing_shortage" title="Nursing shortage">nursing shortage</a> and is expected<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2012)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to increase for the foreseeable future. There are data to support the idea that the nursing shortage is a voluntary shortage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In other words, nurses are leaving nursing of their own volition. In 2006 it was estimated that approximately 1.8 million nurses chose not to work as a nurse. The Bureau of Labor Statistics (BLS) reported that 296,900 healthcare jobs were created in 2011. RNs make up the majority of the healthcare work force, therefore these positions will be filled primarily by nurses. The BLS also states that by 2020, there will be 1.2 million nursing job openings due to an increase in the workforce, and replacements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h6><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nursing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h6>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em
